    Syntheses and Evaluation of New Bisacridine Derivatives for Dual Binding of G-Quadruplex and i-Motif in Regulating Oncogene c-myc Expression

    Abstract

    The c-myc oncogene is an important regulator for cell growth and differentiation, and its aberrant overexpression is closely related to the occurrence and development of various cancers. Thus, the suppression of c-myc transcription and expression has been investigated for cancer treatment. In this study, various new bisacridine derivatives were synthesized and evaluated for their binding with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif. We found that a9 could bind to and stabilize both G-quadruplex and i-motif, resulting in the downregulation of c-myc gene transcription. a9 could inhibit cancer cell proliferation and induce SiHa cell apoptosis and cycle arrest. a9 exhibited tumor growth inhibition activity in a SiHa xenograft tumor model, which might be related to its binding with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif. Our results suggested that a9 as a dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binder could be effective in both oncogene replication and transcription and become a promising lead compound for further development with improved potency and selectivity.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The human c-myc proto-oncogene is an important regulator of a wide array of cellular processes necessary for cell growth and differentiation, and its dysregulation is one of the hallmarks of many cancers.(1â4) The aberrant overexpression of the c-myc gene is closely related to the occurrence and development of various human cancers.(3,5) Therefore, the strategies for suppression of c-myc transcription and prevention of the C-MYC protein from binding to its relative proteins or genes in established cancer cell lines have been well studied for cancer treatment.(3,4,6â9) It has been shown that the crystal structure of the C-MYC protein does not have a cavity that permits small molecules to bind easily, so it is very complicated to simulate and design direct inhibitors.(9,10) Thus, the development of effective methods for downregulating c-myc gene transcription by genetic silencing has become a practical and promising therapeutic strategy.(11)
    The nuclease hypersensitive element III1 (NHE III1), located upstream of the P1 promoter of the c-myc gene, controls 85â90% of c-myc transcription.(4,12,13) The region is guanine/cytosine rich (GC-rich) and contains two nuclease hypersensitive sites. As a result of transcription-induced negative superhelicity during cellular processes, the GC-rich region can open up to make the formation of two non-B-form unique DNA secondary structures: G-quadruplexes on the G-rich strand and i-motif on the complementary C-rich strand.(13â15) The c-myc promoter G-quadruplexes have been largely studied and previously characterized to play key roles as transcriptional repressors in modulating gene transcription, and various ligands binding to and inducing the formation of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex could suppress c-myc gene transcription and expression.(12,16,17) The population of G-quadruplexes has been found to be the lowest at the cell cycle stage G0/G1 (no replication), intermediate at G1/S, and maximal during the S phase (DNA replication) using antibody staining, consistent with the cellular-replication-dependent formation of G-quadruplexes.(18) It should be noted that G-rich sequences widely exist in promoter regions of the human genome, which could be folded into G-quadruplexes. Therefore, selective recognition and stabilization of small-molecule-binding ligands is a problem. One possible solution is to have their corresponding C-rich strands into consideration, which could form i-motif structures and influence the stability of corresponding G-quadruplexes.
    However, the studies of i-motif structures are rarely reported. The c-myc gene promoter C-rich strand at the NHE III1 region could form the i-motif structure, which comprises two parallel duplexes with intercalated hemiprotonated cytosine+âcytosine (C+âC) base pairs.(6,19,20) The i-motif structure can form under slightly acidic conditions or at neutral pH under molecular crowding conditions, and it is more complex than the G-quadruplex in the complementary strand.(21â25) Recent studies suggest the existence of the i-motif structure in cells and its important roles in gene regulation, which also make it an attractive target for anticancer drug development and gene regulation processes.(19,22,23,25â28) In addition, the formation of i-motifs varied over the cell cycle, with the highest level of formation occurring during the late G1 phase, which is characterized by high levels of transcription and cellular growth. This is significantly different from G-quadruplex formation, which occurs predominately during the DNA replication S phase, suggesting that the occurrence of i-motifs and G-quadruplexes is generally independent and the i-motif regulation in the cell cycle might be more important than the G-quadruplex regulation.(27) In addition, it has been shown that i-motifs are better replication inhibitors than mixed-type G-quadruplexes or hairpin structures, even though all of them have similar thermodynamic stabilities.(29) To date, several i-motif-binding ligands have been characterized, including porphyrin (TMPYP4),(30,31)PBP1-2,(32)acridone,(33)phenanthroline,(34) and mitoxantrone(6) (Figure 1). It has been reported that PBP1 could bind to and induce the formation of the bcl-2 promoter i-motif to upregulate gene transcription and expression. In comparison, PBP2 could bind to and induce the formation of the bcl-2 promoter G-quadruplex and downregulate gene transcription and expression. In a previous study, we have reported that an acridone derivative could selectively bind to and induce c-myc promoter i-motif formation, resulting in the downregulation of c-myc transcription. These findings indicated that the c-myc promoter i-motif could play an important role as a transcriptional regulator in c-myc gene transcription. It should be noted that drug resistance could happen if a molecule binds to only one molecular target and therefore a multifunctional molecule binding to more than one target especially in different phases of the cell cycle could overcome drug resistance and become much effective. Presently, small molecules binding to both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex (cellular S phase-arrested) and i-motif (late G1 phase-interfered) could be much efficient in downregulating gene transcription with improved selectivity.
    To find small molecular ligands targeting both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif structures, we screened our synthesized compound library in our laboratory using the fluorescence resonance energy transfer (FRET) melting assay. We found that an acridine derivative could stabilize both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif structures. Then, we designed and synthesized a variety of structural analogues and derivatives and found that a bisacridine derivative (a5) could increase the stability of both G-quadruplex and i-motif, with decreased stability of duplex and hairpin structures. We further synthesized 24 bisacridine analogues and evaluated their stability and binding affinity to both G-quadruplex and i-motif. Among these compounds, a9 was found to be a potent dual binding ligand stabilizing both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif. Our in vitro and cellular experiments confirmed that a9 could interact with both G-quadruplex and i-motif structures. Our further studies suggested that a9 could hinder cell migration and repress tumor growth possibly through downregulating c-myc gene transcription. These findings illustrated that stabilization of both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif structures with a9 could repress c-myc gene transcription and expression much effectively and selectively, which could offer a significantly improved strategy for controlling tumor cell growth and migration.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Screening of Small Molecules for Binding to c-myc Promoter G-Quadruplex and i-Motif

    To study dual G-quadruplex/i-motif interactive compounds, a FRET melting experiment was performed to screen our compound library containing about 80 compounds, including acridone derivatives, quinoline derivatives, acridine derivatives, and imidazole derivatives (Supporting Information, Figure S1), with a dual-labeled DNA oligomer containing fluorophores at both 5â²-end and 3â²-end (Table S1). Compounds that could increase the melting temperature (ÎTm) by more than 5 Â°C were considered as potential hits. We found that quinoline derivatives, imidazole derivatives, indole derivatives, and acridone derivatives (B01â12) did not significantly increase the melting temperatures of i-motif or G-quadruplex. Acridone derivatives (B13â24) could stabilize c-myc promoter i-motif more significantly than G-quadruplex. Acridine derivatives (AD01â16) could increase the stability of both G-quadruplex and i-motif, which were regarded as hit compounds in this study (Figure S1). Among these acridine derivatives, N1-(2,7-dimethylacridin-9-yl)-N2,N2-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine (AD06) could significantly stabilize both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif structures with its ÎTm values of 8.3 and 10.0 Â°C, respectively, at 5 Î¼M concentration. However, AD06 could also stabilize the hairpin structure with a ÎTm value of 6.8 Â°C, indicating its possible DNA intercalative effect. It has been reported that a molecule containing two acridine units connected to a linker of two diethylenetriamines (named BisA) could increase the melting temperatures of the G-quadruplex and i-motif much more significantly than a monomeric acridine substituted with two propylaminomethyl groups (named MonoA).(35) This result indicated that two or more pharmacophores connected with a single-chain amine linker could have a higher binding affinity, possibly through increased molecular interactions. Therefore, we designed and synthesized a new compound having two acridine units connected to a single-chain amine linker (BisA01). We found that BisA01 could stabilize both G-quadruplex and i-motif with the ÎTm values of 6.2 and 16.0 Â°C, respectively. In comparison, BisA01 had decreased stability to the hairpin structure (ÎTm = 2.5 Â°C), indicating that BisA01 might have a less intercalative effect to double-strand DNA, possibly due to its increased molecular size. This could increase the selectivity of the hit compound for G-quadruplex and i-motif with relatively less side effects or toxicity. These inspiring data encouraged us to further synthesize BisA01 analogues for optimizing the activity.

    Syntheses of Bisacridine Derivatives

    Since BisA01 was found as a hit compound with dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binding affinity and improved selectivity, we further synthesized various analogues for optimizing binding interactions with the G-quadruplex and i-motif. The two acridine rings of BisA01 could possibly stack on the external 5â²-terminal G-quartet through ÏâÏ stacking interactions or interact with the loops of G-quadruplex/i-motif. The linker of two acridine rings could interact with the grooves of G-quadruplex/i-motif through electrostatic interactions with a negatively charged phosphate diester backbone. In addition, polyamine chains could facilitate molecular entrances for their optimal delivery to target via polyamine transport system (PTS), especially for drugs interacting with DNA, to cause cellular DNA damage and cell apoptosis.(36â38) In the present study, we designed and synthesized various bisacridine derivatives linked with different amine chains (a5â24), as shown in Figure 2 and Scheme 1. It should be mentioned that several i-motif-binding ligands have substituted amine side chains as shown in Figure 1, which could increase their stability and binding affinity to promoter i-motif. We have previously reported that acridone derivatives linked with benzoyl amine side chains had increased binding affinity to c-myc promoter i-motif. Thus, in this study, we introduced two benzoyl amine side chains to bisacridine derivatives for compounds a25â26. For comparison, we introduced two ester groups to bisacridine derivatives for compounds a27â28.
    The synthetic pathway for bisacridine derivatives a5â28 is shown in Scheme 1. Benzoic acid 1 as a starting material was reacted with bromobenzene to give intermediate 2 as a crude mixture, which was used for the next step without purification. The intermediate 2 reacted in concentrated sulfuric acid through the FriedelâCrafts acylation reaction to produce intermediate acridone 3, which was then reacted with phosphorus oxychloride immediately to generate acridine derivative 4. In addition, crude intermediate 6 was obtained through the reaction of benzoic acid 5 with 4-bromobenzoic acid instead of bromobenzene in a similar condition, which was followed with an intracyclization reaction in concentrated sulfuric acid and then reaction with ethanol to generate acridone 7. The intermediate 7 was hydrolyzed to give an acid 8 under basic conditions, followed by treatment with SOCl2 to generate its acyl chloride and then reaction with N1,N1-dimethylpropane-1,3-diamine or MeOH to give key intermediates 9 and 10, respectively. At last, the acridine intermediates 4, 9, and 10 reacted with NH2-L-NH2 in phenol to give the target compounds a5â28. All synthesized intermediates and compounds were purified, and their structures were determined using high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC), NMR, and high resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS). The purified compounds a5â28 were used for subsequent biophysical and biochemical as well as cell-based studies.

    StructureâActivity Relationship Studies

    The binding affinities of the synthesized bisacridine derivatives for c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif were studied through a FRET melting experiment to evaluate their thermal stability changes, and the results are shown in Table 1 and Figure S2. All synthesized bisacridine derivatives with a long alkyl amine linker could increase the stability of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex (FPu22T) with ÎTm values ranging from 7.7 to 17.1 Â°C. Among these bisacridine derivatives, a6â10 with a relatively long alkyl amine linker could increase the melting temperature (4.5â11.8 Â°C) of c-myc promoter i-motif (FPy33T), suggesting that a long alkyl amine linker could be a favorable factor. a25â26 with benzoyl amide side chains could also increase the melting temperature of c-myc promoter i-motif with ÎTm values of 27.4 and 24.3 Â°C, respectively. It is possible that the benzoyl amide side chains could participate in hydrogen-bonding interactions with the i-motif to increase stabilization.(33)

    Then, we studied the binding affinity and selectivity of our synthesized bisacridine derivatives to c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif through the surface plasmon resonance (SPR) method. The binding constant KD values for the binding affinity of the bisacridine derivatives to G-quadruplex, i-motif, a C-rich single-strand oligomer, and hairpin structures were determined as shown in Table 2 and Figure S3. As a control experiment, their corresponding monomeric acridines with AD06â08 as examples showed no significant interaction with the DNA. All synthesized bisacridine derivatives with long alkyl amine linkers could bind to the G-quadruplex structure formed by Pu27 with KD values ranging from 1.1 to 18.3 Î¼M. Compounds a5, a11â24 with relatively few amine functional groups on the linkers showed relatively weak i-motif-binding affinity. Compounds a6â10 with relatively more amine functional groups on the linkers could bind well to c-myc promoter i-motif with their KD values ranging from 2.4 to 9.1 Î¼M, suggesting that the amines on the linkers could be favorable for their binding affinity to c-myc promoter i-motif.

    No significant binding was found for the addition of up to 50 Î¼M ligand, which might indicate no specific interaction between the ligand and the DNA.

    Next, carboxyl amide side chains were introduced to compounds a11 and a9 to give a25 and a26, which showed increased binding affinity to c-myc promoter i-motif with KD values of 8.5 and 1.0 Î¼M, respectively, indicating their favorable effect for binding interactions with the i-motif. In addition, these compounds generally had a relatively high binding affinity to the c-myc promoter i-motif structure rather than the C-rich single-strand oligonucleotide at pH 7.4 (Table 2 and Figure S3). In addition, we found that most bisacridine derivatives with various R1 and R2 substitutive groups showed relatively weak binding affinity to double-strand DNA. In comparison, a5, a10, a13, and a24 without a substituent showed a relatively high binding affinity to double-stranded DNA. This might indicate that R1 and R2 substitutive groups could disturb their interactions with double-stranded DNA. Generally, our structureâactivity relationship data for their binding to G-quadruplex and i-motif were consistent with those for their stability to G-quadruplex and i-motif. Among our synthetic compounds, a9 and a26 exhibited much better binding affinity (Table 2 and Figure S3) than the hit compound BisA01. Our FRET and SPR results all showed that our bisacridine derivatives with substitutive groups had generally weak binding affinity and stability to duplex DNA, indicating their insignificant intercalative effect compared with their binding affinity to c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif.

    In-Depth Studies of Selected Bisacridine Derivatives

    On the basis of the above data, compounds a9 and a26 had relatively high ÎTm values and strong binding affinity, which were selected for further studies with other compounds as controls for comparison. Their interactions with G-quadruplex/i-motif were studied through circular dichroism (CD) spectroscopy, isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC), NMR, and native polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis (PAGE) for further verification.
    CD spectroscopy has been used previously as a sensitive method to determine the effects of various ligands on the conformations of G-quadruplex/i-motif under different conditions.(6,23) The oligomer Pu27 (Table S1) was used as the c-myc G-quadruplex-forming sequence. As shown in Figure 3A, in the presence of 100 mM KCl, Pu27 showed a positive peak at 260â265 nm and a negative peak at near 240 nm, which suggested the formation of a typical parallel G-quadruplex structure. In the absence of potassium ions, its CD spectrum is similar to that in the presence of KCl but with much lower signal intensity. After treatment with different bisacridine derivatives in the absence of potassium ions, we found that some compounds increased the Pu27 CD signal intensity, while some others did not affect or decrease its signal intensity (Figure S4). Generally, the bisacridines with a linker containing an amine group, alkyl group, or oxygen atom could maintain or increase the CD signal intensity, especially the derivatives with a linker containing an oxygen atom (a11â13, a27) (Figure 3A). This might indicate that these compounds could effectively induce the formation of a parallel G-quadruplex in the absence of metal ions.
    Then, we also studied the effects of our bisacridines on c-myc promoter i-motif using CD spectroscopy. Oligomer Py33 showed a positive peak at 287â290 nm and a negative peak near 260 nm at pH 5.5, which indicated the formation of an i-motif structure.(6,23,39) For comparison as a control at pH 7.0, the CD spectra exhibited the feature of a random coil (blue) with only a positive peak near 270 nm (Figure 3B).(40â43)a9â10 and a25â26 had relatively high stability and strong binding affinity to the i-motif, which decreased both the positive peak at 288 nm and the negative peak at 260 nm without switching to a random coil characterized with 270 nm in CD spectroscopy (Figure 3B), indicating that these compounds could cause some conformational change on the c-myc promoter i-motif structure. We performed a CD melting experiment for a9, a11, and a26. Our results showed that a9 and a26 stabilized both G-quadruplex and i-motif, while a11 stabilized G-quadruplex only (Table S3, Figure S5), which were consistent with our FRET melting results.
    1H NMR spectroscopy is also a common method to characterize the conformation of G-quadruplex/i-motif structures.(14,39) We studied the effects of a9 on c-myc promoter i-motif/G-quadruplex using 1H NMR spectroscopy. The hemi-protonated CâC+ base-pair imino proton peaks of oligomer Py33 were at 15â16 ppm, which indicated the formation of c-myc promoter i-motif. Upon titration with an increasing concentration of a9, the i-motif signals at 15â16 ppm were gradually increased (Figure S6B). This result showed that a9 could induce the formation of an i-motif structure. The Hoogsteen hydrogen imino proton peaks of oligomer Pu27 were at 10.5â12 ppm, which indicated the formation of G-quadruplex. Compound a9 increased the G-quadruplex signal at 10.5â12 ppm (Figure S6B), indicating that a9 could induce the formation of a G-quadruplex structure.
    ITC is a method to determine the binding affinity of two in-solution biomolecules.(44) Here, we used ITC to study the binding of a9 to c-myc promoter i-motif or G-quadruplex. As shown in Figure S6A, a9 bound to c-myc promoter i-motif with a KD value of 0.65 Î¼M and bound to c-myc promoter G-quadruplex with a KD value of 0.81 Î¼M. These results showed that a9 bound tightly with c-myc promoter i-motif and G-quadruplex, which were consistent with our SPR results.
    To further confirm the binding affinity of a9 and a26 to c-myc promoter i-motif, native PAGE electrophoresis experiments were performed, which have been previously used to study the binding interactions of ligands with i-motif structures.(14,45) Oligonucleotide Py33 could form different DNA structures at different pH conditions. Generally, under weakly acidic conditions, Py33 could aggregate and fold into an i-motif DNA secondary structure, resulting in larger molecular tightness and redistribution of peripheral charge, mainly represented by positive charges located at the center of the i-motif, while negative charges lay in the phosphate backbone region, producing a slower migration band in native PAGE gel.(14) However, under nearly neutral conditions, Py33 could unfold into an unordered random coil, which migrated faster than i-motif. In this study, Py33 was co-annealed with different concentrations of a9 and a26 in 1Ã BPES buffer (pH 6.6), and the obtained mixture samples were stored at 4 Â°C overnight, separated through electrophoreses, and then silver-stained. As shown in Figure 3C, after the addition of an increasing concentration of a9 and a26, a slower migration band was formed in a dose-dependent manner, indicating that a9 and a26 could bind to Py33 and possibly induce its transformation from a random coil to an i-motif structure.
    Furthermore, we studied the selectivity of a9 on different G-quadruplex or i-motif structures using CD melting, UV titration, and SPR experiments. The melting temperatures for promoter i-motif or G-quadruplex oligomers from some other genes (bcl-2, c-kit, platelet-derived growth factor (PDGF), telomere, vascular endothelial growth factor (VEGF)) were determined in the presence and absence of a9. Compound a9 exhibited the highest ÎTm values for c-myc promoter i-motif and G-quadruplex in comparison with other oligomers (Table S4, Figure S5). Compound a9 also exhibited the lowest KD value for c-myc promoter i-motif and G-quadruplex measured through UV titration and SPR experiments, indicating good binding selectivity of a9 to c-myc promoter i-motif and G-quadruplex (Figure S5). These results indicated that a9 had good selectivity and stability to c-myc promoter i-motif and G-quadruplex structures over other gene promoter secondary structures.

    Bisacridine Derivatives Inhibited Carcinoma Cells Proliferation

    To investigate the antitumor activity of the derivatives, we employed the 3-(4,5-dimethylthiazol-2-yl)-2,5-diphenyltetrazolium bromide (MTT) assay to evaluate the antiproliferative activity of our bisacridine derivatives on various human cancer cell lines, such as the human melanoma cell line A375, the human squamous cervical cancer cell line Hela, the human lung adenocarcinoma cell line A549, the human bone osteosarcoma epithelial cell line U2OS, the human colon cancer cell line HCT116, the human cervical cancer cell line SiHa, and the human hepatocellular carcinoma cell line HuH7. The cytotoxicity of the bisacridine derivatives a5â28 was determined with their IC50 values as shown in Table 3. Most of the bisacridine derivatives showed diverse cytotoxicity against various tumor cells. Generally, their activity of inhibiting tumor cell growth was well consistent with their ability to bind to and stabilize G-quadruplex and i-motif structures. The compounds interacted with both G-quadruplex and i-motif such as a6â10, showing potent inhibition of proliferation of tumor cells. a25â26 exhibited strong stabilizing ability and binding affinity to c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif, however, with low cytotoxicity. These two compounds with relatively high molecular weights are substituted with easily protonated amine side chains, which could possibly affect their membrane permeability. Indeed, our cell uptake assays (Figure S7) showed that cellular uptakes of a25â26 were much lower than those of compounds a9 and a11. Based on the above results, a9 could stabilize c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif with high ÎTm values, bind the G-quadruplex/i-motif with submicromolar binding constants, and exhibit strong inhibitory effects on the proliferation of tumor cells and was therefore selected as a promising compound for our further investigation.

    Regulation of c-myc Transcription through Interaction with Its Promoter G-Quadruplex and i-Motif

    As mentioned above, a9 appeared to be a potent binding ligand to both G-quadruplex and i-motif. a11 binding to and inducing the formation of c-myc G-quadruplex only without a significant effect on i-motif was used for comparison. To investigate and compare effects of these derivatives on c-myc transcription, several experiments were performed, including the dual-luciferase reporter assay, reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction (RT-PCR), and western blotting.
    The dual-luciferase reporter assay was performed with a psicheck2 plasmid carrying the c-myc promoter in front of the Renilla Firefly luciferase gene. After transfection, SiHa cells were incubated with the bisacridine derivatives at a concentration of about 1/3 of IC50 value for 48 h. As shown in Figure S8A, a9 could more significantly decrease the ratio of Renilla/Firefly luciferase activity than a11. It should be noted that some other compounds binding to G-quadruplex only did not show good activity in the dual-luciferase reporter assay either, which is possibly due to complicated regulatory mechanisms of cells. To identify whether this inhibitory activity was due to its interaction with G-quadruplex/i-motif, we further constructed a plasmid carrying the mutant and deleted c-myc promoter, which could not form G-quadruplex/i-motif structures and had no NHE III1 G/C-rich DNA sequence, respectively. Upon treatment with increasing concentration of a9, a dose-dependent decrease of the ratio of Renilla/Firefly luciferase activity was observed for the wild-type plasmid but not for the mutant or deleted plasmid (Figure S8B), suggesting that a9 presumably interacted with the c-myc G/C-rich DNA sequence to affect Renilla/Firefly luciferase activity.
    Reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction (RT-PCR) was performed to determine the effect of a9 on the regulation of c-myc gene transcription in cells. a11 binding to and inducing c-myc G-quadruplex formation only was again used for comparison. SiHa cells were incubated with a9 and a11 at increasing concentrations of 0, 1.25, 2.5, and 5.0 Î¼M for 3 h. As shown in Figure 4A, the treatment of the cells with a9 and a11 both caused reductions of c-myc mRNA levels in dose-dependent manners in SiHa cells, and a9 obviously had more a significant effect on transcriptional regulation. The levels of c-myc mRNA were reduced by up to 40.1, 76.9, and 92.7%, relative to a Î²-actin control with the treatment of 1.25, 2.5 and 5.0 Î¼M a9, while the same concentrations of a11 reduced the mRNA levels by only 32.6, 55.8, and 79.8%, respectively. Since c-myc mRNA also contains G-quadruplex on its 5â²UTR, it is necessary to know whether a9 could affect the stability of c-myc mRNA. Actinomycin D is a known antibiotic inhibiting mRNA synthesis.(46) Our incubation of SiHa cells with actinomycin D in the presence and absence of a9 showed almost the same degradation velocity of c-myc mRNA (Figure S9), indicating that a9 had no significant effect on the stability of c-myc mRNA.
    To further identify whether this transcriptional inhibition happened through the interaction of a9 with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif-forming sequence, Raji and CA46 cells (two Burkittâs lymphoma cell lines) were comparatively incubated with a9, which has been regarded as a necessary experiment to determine whether a ligand is targeting the c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif sequence in cells.(47â49) As reported previously, the c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif forming sequence could exist in the fragment of nontranslocated chromosomes in Raji cells but not in translocated chromosomes in CA46 cells. If the mechanism of c-myc transcriptional modulation is mediated through its promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif sequence, a preferential change should be evident with a functional MYC under the control (Figure S10A).(50,51) As shown in Figure S10B, a9 produced a dose-dependent transcriptional downregulation of c-myc in Raji cells. In comparison, the treatment of CA46 cells with an increasing concentration of a9 did not cause a reduction of c-myc mRNA in a dose-dependent manner (Figure S10C). These results indicated that a9 could directly target the c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif-forming sequence in cells.
    Western blotting was also carried out to measure C-MYC protein expression levels, as shown in Figure 4B. The expression levels of C-MYC were reduced by up to 39.8, 73.9 and 92.2% relative to a GAPDH control upon treatment with 1.25, 2.5, and 5.0 Î¼M a9, respectively. In comparison, upon treatment with the same concentrations of a11, C-MYC protein levels were reduced by only 25.3, 42.2, and 62.3%, respectively. These results showed that upon incubation with increasing concentrations of compounds a9 and a11, the expression levels of C-MYC decreased in dose-dependent manners. a9 as a dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binder could downregulate c-myc gene expression more effectively than a11 that interacted with G-quadruplex only.

    Effect of Compound a9 on Cancer Cells Apoptosis and Cycle Arrest

    Since c-myc is a key oncogene related to several cellular events, the suppression of oncogenic c-myc transcription and expression by small molecules has a significant antitumor effect on various types of tumor cells. Our above studies indicated that a9 could bind to both G-quadruplex and i-motif of the c-myc promoter, resulting in the downregulation of c-myc transcription and translation. Therefore, we further studied its antitumor activity with other biophysical and biochemical methods. As mentioned above, a9 could inhibit SiHa cell short-term proliferation with an IC50 value of 1.22 Î¼M. Its inhibitory effect on cell proliferation was further investigated through the colony formation assay and the transwell assay to study its inhibition of long-term proliferation and invasion of tumor cells, respectively. As shown in Figure S11A, upon incubation with a9, colony formation obviously decreased, suggesting inhibition of cell proliferation, which was consistent with our MTT results. For comparison, we used pyridostatin (PDS) as a well-known G-quadruplex-stabilizing ligand(52) to study and compare the effects of G-quadruplex ligands on cell proliferation, and a9 showed obviously a more significant effect on SiHa cell colony formation. Then, SiHa cells were incubated with 0.5 Î¼M a9 for 24 h, and the cell migration was reduced by approximately 85% as shown in Figure S11B, indicating that a9 could inhibit SiHa cell invasion. The above results suggested that a9 could inhibit the proliferation and invasion of cancer cells, which could be due to its repression of c-myc transcription.
    It has been previously reported that the decreased expression of c-myc could induce the cellular apoptosis and cell cycle arrest of proliferating cancer cells.(53,54) Therefore, we investigated whether a9 could induce the cellular apoptosis and cell cycle arrest of SiHa cells through the downregulation of c-myc transcription. SiHa cells treated with a9 were analyzed using flow cytometry. As shown in Figure 5, the G2/M phase cell cycle arrest was significant for SiHa cells treated with a9 for 24 h, compared with the control group. The cells were arrested at the G2/M phase by up to 56.9% upon treatment with 0.6 Î¼M a9. In addition, a sub-G0 peak was observed upon treatment with 0.6 Î¼M a9. SiHa cells treated with or without a9 were examined for apoptosis using propidium iodide (PI) and annexin V staining. As shown in Figure 6A,B, upon incubation of SiHa cells with a9 at increasing concentrations of 0, 0.6, 1.2, and 2.4 Î¼M for 24 h, both early cell apoptosis and late cell apoptosis were induced. The percentages of early apoptotic cells were 0.3, 18.1, 43.2, and 55.4%, while the percentages of late apoptotic cells were 0.4, 3.8, 18.7, and 27.5%, respectively. Our results indicated that a9 could induce the SiHa cell cycle arrest at the G2/M phase and apoptosis, possibly through the repression of c-myc transcription.
    Since the activation of caspases is an important process during cell apoptosis, the detection of apoptosis markers such as caspase 7, cleaved caspase 7, caspase 9, and cleaved caspase 9 in SiHa cells upon treatment with a9 was carried out using western blotting. As shown in Figure 6B, upon treatment with increasing concentrations of a9, the levels of caspase 7 and caspase 9 decreased in dose-dependent manners. On the contrary, cleaved caspase 7 and cleaved caspase 9 showed a dose-dependent increase upon treatment with a9, suggesting that a9 induced apoptosis possibly through decreasing the levels of active caspases 7 and 9 while activating cleaved caspase 7 and cleaved caspase 9 in cancer cells.
    In addition, we studied and compared the effects of a9 and PDS on cell proliferation and apoptosis and cell cycle distribution for Raji and CA46 cells. PDS has been shown to silence a series of oncogenes including c-myc and SRC, which showed inhibition on both Raji and CA46 cells in this study. In contrast, a9 inhibited only Raji cell proliferation and induced Raji cell apoptosis and cycle arrest at the G2/M phase, without a significant effect on CA46 cells (Table S5, Figures S12 and S13). These results further indicated that a9 repressed tumor cell growth through the downregulation of c-myc transcription.

    Compound a9 Inhibited Tumor Growth in a SiHa Cervical Xenograft

    Since the above results showed that a9 could downregulate c-myc gene transcription and inhibit tumor cell growth, its antitumor activity was further evaluated against a human cervical cancer xenograft (SiHa) in athymic nude mice, as shown in Figure 7AâC. An experimental group of six tumor-bearing mice was treated with a9 once 2 days at a dosage of 15 mg/kg for 18 days. No significant change in body weight was observed during the treatment with a9 (Figure 7A). The negative control group, which was treated with saline only, had an average tumor volume of 750 mm3 after 18 days. In contrast, the tumor-bearing mice treated with a9 had an average tumor volume of 450 mm3. The tumor weight in the presence of a9 reduced by around 41.6% to a final weight of 0.55g (Figure 7B). The positive control group was treated once 2 days with cisplatin at a dosage of 0.2 mg/kg for 18 days, with the average tumor volume reduced to 200 mm3 and the average tumor weight reduced to 0.18 g. These results suggested that a9 had good antitumor activity on the SiHa-xenografted mice. The effect of compound a9 on various organs was also assessed as shown in Figure 7D, and no significant change was observed. This indicated that a9 could have high selectivity to tumor cells without significant toxicity to the normal body in comparison with cisplatin.

    Molecular Docking Studies for the Binding of Compound a9 to c-myc Promoter G-Quadruplex and i-Motif

    Molecular docking studies were performed to understand possible interactions of a9 with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif structures using the MOE program. a9 was docked to the c-myc promoter G-quadruplex (PDB ID: 2l7v) structure. However, either the NMR or the X-ray crystallographic structure of c-myc promoter i-motif is not available in the literature and therefore telomeric i-motif DNA (PDB ID: 1eln) was used as a model structure for analyzing possible binding interactions of a9 with i-motif. As shown in Figure 8A, our docking result for the interaction of a9 with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex showed that one acridine ring could stack on the external 5â²-terminal G-quartet through a ÏâÏ stacking interaction, and another acridine backbone could possibly lay on the DNA groove space. a9 should be positively charged in physiological conditions, which could interact with the negatively charged phosphate diester backbone of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex through electrostatic interactions. The amine linker could form two important H-bonds with the backbone of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex with distances of 3.3 and 3.5 Çº. Our docking result for the interaction of a9 with c-myc promoter i-motif is shown in Figure 8B. Our docking result showed that both acridine rings of a9 could interact with i-motif through ÏâÏ stacking interactions. Similar to the binding interaction of a9 with c-myc promoter G-quadruplex, a9 could interact with i-motif through electrostatic interactions and H-bonds with distances of 3.2 Çº. Our UV-titration data showed that the absorption peak of a9 shifted toward a longer wavelength in the presence of G-quadruplex or i-motif, indicating the ÏâÏ stacking interactions between a9 and G-quadruplex or i-motif (Figure S14). This result was in accordance with the docking study. These results further reinforced our design to have an acridine dimer for interacting with both G-quadruplex and i-motif.

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    It should be noted that previous efforts have largely been focused on the study of G-quadruplex-binding ligands, while i-motif-binding ligands have been much less studied. In this study, we explored a new strategy of targeting both G-quadruplex and i-motif on the c-myc oncogene promoter for transcriptional regulation through the syntheses and evaluation of disubstituted bisacridine derivatives. FRET, NMR, SPR, ITC, and CD experiments were carried out to study the effects of different disubstituted derivatives with various chain linkers on binding to and stabilization of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif. Our FRET and SPR results showed that our bisacridine derivatives with substitutive groups had generally weak binding and stability to duplex DNA, indicating their insignificant intercalating effect compared with their binding affinity to c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif. Compound a9 was found to be a potent dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binder and selected for further biological evaluation. a9 could downregulate c-myc gene transcription and expression, possibly through stabilization of c-myc promoter G-quadruplex/i-motif. Our cellular experiments showed that a9 exhibited antiproliferative effects on cancer cells by inducing cell apoptosis and cycle arrest. Its antiproliferative effect was further evaluated with a cervical cancer xenograft model, indicating its high selectivity to SiHa cancer cells.
    The population of G-quadruplexes has been found to be maximal in the S phase of the cell cycle using antibody staining, consistent with the cellular-replication-dependent formation of G-quadruplexes. The formation of i-motifs varied over the cell cycle with the highest level occurring in the late G1 phase, which is characterized by high levels of transcription and cellular growth. The G-quadruplex and i-motif of the c-myc oncogene promoter may serve as a dual regulating system to securely lock down oncogene transcription and expression. Our present study showed that a9 as a dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binder could downregulate c-myc gene transcription and induce cell apoptosis more effectively than a11, which interacted with G-quadruplex only. a9 as a dual G-quadruplex/i-motif binder could be effective in both oncogene replication and transcription, while a11 could be effective mainly in the oncogene replication stage. a9 could become a promising lead compound for further development for cancer treatment, targeting both c-myc promoter G-quadruplex and i-motif with improved potency and selectivity. Our present results increased our understanding of a gene regulation system and shed light on the development of improved anticancer drugs.

    General Methods for Syntheses

    All chemicals were purchased from commercial sources and used as starting materials and were of analytical grade without further purification unless otherwise specified. All synthesized compounds were characterized using 1H and 13C NMR spectra and HRMS. 1H and 13C NMR spectra were recorded using TMS as the internal standard in DMSO-d6, CD3OD, or CDCl3 with a Bruker BioSpin GmbH spectrometer at 400 and 100 MHz, respectively. High-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) was performed on a Shimadzu LCMS-IT-TOF of MAT95XP mass spectrometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific). The purity of the synthesized compound was confirmed to be higher than 95% using analytical HPLC performed with a dual-pump Shimadzu LC-20 AB system equipped with an Ultimate XB-C18 column (4.6 mm Ã 250 mm, 5 Î¼M), eluting with methanolâwater (10:90 to 60:40) containing 0.05% trifluoroacetic acid (TFA) at a flow rate of 0.5 mL/min.
    The intermediates were prepared following the process shown in Scheme 1.

    Synthesis of 5-Substituted-2-(4-substituted-phenylamino)benzoic Acid (2)

    To a solution of 2-amino-5-substituted-benzoic acid (1, 19.87 mmol) in anhydrous dimethylformamide (50 mL) were added 1-bromo-4-substituted-benzene (59.6 mmol, 3 equiv), anhydrous potassium carbonate (4.11 g, 29.8 mmol), copper powder (300 mg), and copper iodide (100 mg). The mixture was stirred and heated under reflux and a nitrogen atmosphere and monitored using thin-layer chromatography (TLC). After being cooled down, the mixture was poured into ice water and stirred, with pH adjusted to 3â4. Ethyl acetate (100 mL) was added and stirred for another 10 min and filtered, and the residue was extracted with ethyl acetate (3 Ã 50 mL). The combined organic layer was washed with diluted hydrochloric acid and then brine three times, dried over anhydrous sodium sulfate, filtered, and concentrated under reduced pressure to give crude compound 2, which was used for the next step without purification.

    Synthesis of 2-Substituted-7-substituted-acridin-9(10H)-one (3)

    To a 100 mL round bottom flask containing residue 2 (4.0 g) was added 10 mL of concentrated sulfuric acid. The mixture was stirred at 100 Â°C under a nitrogen atmosphere and monitored using TLC. After being cooled down, the reaction mixture was poured into ice water and stirred and filtered, and the solid was washed with saturated sodium bicarbonate and dried to give crude compound 3, which was used for the next step without purification.

    Synthesis of 9-Chloro-2-substituted-7-substituted-acridine (4)

    To a 100 mL round-bottom flask containing residue 3 (3.0 g) were added POCl3 (10 mL) dropwise and DMF (0.1 mL) at 0â5 Â°C. The reaction mixture was stirred for 30 min and then heated to 100 Â°C slowly with stirring, which was monitored until the completion of the reaction using TLC. After the mixture was cooled down, the solvent was removed, and the residue was poured into ice water with stirring. Ethyl acetate (50 mL) was added, and the pH was adjusted to 9â10 with sodium bicarbonate. The resulting solution was filtered, and the water phase was extracted with ethyl acetate (20 mL Ã 3). The combined organic layer was washed with brine three times, dried over anhydrous sodium sulfate, filtered, and then concentrated under reduced pressure. The residue was purified using chromatograpy on silica gel with petroleum ether/ethyl acetate (10/1â2/1) to give the desire intermediate 4, with an overall yield of 20â30% in three steps.
    9-Chloro-2,7-dimethylacridine (4a), yellow solid, yield 30%. 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3) Î´ 8.15 (s, 2H), 8.10 (d, J = 8.9 Hz, 2H), 7.61 (dd, J = 8.9, 1.8 Hz, 2H), 2.61 (s, 6H). ESI-MS (m/z) 242 [M + H]+.
    9-Chloro-2-methylacridine (4b), yellow solid, yield 20%. 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3) Î´ 8.42 (dd, J = 8.7, 0.5 Hz, 1H), 8.27â8.16 (m, 2H), 8.13 (d, J = 8.9 Hz, 1H), 7.78 (ddd, J = 8.7, 7.9, 4.7 Hz, 1H), 7.69â7.57 (m, 2H), 2.63 (s, 3H). ESI-MS (m/z) 228 [M + H]+.
    9-Chloroacridine (4c), yellow solid, yield 24%. 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3) Î´ 8.11 (dd, J = 7.5, 1.4 Hz, 2H), 8.03 (dd, J = 7.5, 1.6 Hz, 2H), 7.75-7.71 (m, 2H), 7.61-7.57 (m, 2H). ESI-MS (m/z) 228 [M + H]+.

    Synthesis of 2-((4-Carboxyphenyl)amino)-5-methylbenzoic Acid (6)

    Based on the procedure for the preparation of compound 2, with bromobenzene replaced by 4-bromobenzoic acid, a crude intermediate 6 was obtained and used for the next step without purification.

    Synthesis of Ethyl 7-Methyl-9-oxo-9,10-dihydroacridine-2-carboxylate (7)

    To a 100 mL round-bottom flask containing residue 6 (4.0 g, crude) was added 10 mL of concentrated sulfuric acid. The reaction mixture was stirred at 120 Â°C under a nitrogen atmosphere, and monitored using TLC. After the mixture was cooled down, ethanol (50 mL) was added dropwise at 0 Â°C. The mixture was heated under reflux under a nitrogen atmosphere for 2 h. After being cooled down, the reaction mixture was poured into ice water with stirring and filtered, and the solid was washed with saturated sodium bicarbonate, dried, and purified using chromatography on silica gel with DCM/MeOH (80/1â10/1) to give 7 as a yellow solid with an overall yield of 30% in three steps. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO) Î´ 11.98 (s, 1H), 8.81 (d, J = 1.8 Hz, 1H), 8.16 (dd, J = 8.8, 2.0 Hz, 1H), 8.02 (s, 1H), 7.63â7.52 (m, 2H), 7.47 (d, J = 8.4 Hz, 1H), 4.35 (q, J = 7.1 Hz, 2H), 2.43 (s, 3H), 1.37 (t, J = 7.1 Hz, 3H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO) Î´ 176.49, 165.28, 143.45, 138.77, 135.38, 132.71, 131.23, 128.61, 125.17, 121.75, 120.77, 119.43, 117.70, 117.60, 60.59, 20.53, 14.23. ESI-MS (m/z) 282 [M + H]+.

    Synthesis of 7-Methyl-9-oxo-9,10-dihydroacridine-2-carboxylic Acid (8)

    To a solution of 7 (2.0 g, 7.12 mmol) in MeOH (30 mL) was added 5 mL of a 10% sodium hydroxide solution. The mixture was stirred at 60 Â°C and monitored with TLC until the completion of the reaction. After being cooled down, the mixture was concentrated, and 20 mL of ice water was added. The mixture was stirred with pH adjusted to 5â6, filtered, washed with H2O, and dried to give intermediate 8 as a crude solid, which was used for the next step without purification.

    Synthesis of 9-Chloro-N-(3-(dimethylamino)propyl)-7-methylacridine-2-carboxamide (9)

    To a 100 mL round-bottom flask containing intermediate 8 (400 mg, 1.58 mmol) was added 5 mL of SOCl2 dropwise followed by three drops of DMF at 0â5 Â°C. The reaction mixture was heated to 80 Â°C slowly and reacted for 2 h. After the mixture was cooled down to room temperature, the solvent was evaporated and the residue was dissolved in trichloromethane (TCM; 10 mL). The reaction mixture was cooled to 0 Â°C, and then N1,N1-dimethylpropane-1,3-diamine (322 mg, 3.16 mmol) and TEA (638 mg, 6.32 mmol) were added with stirring for 1 h. The reaction mixture was quenched and poured into ice water with stirring and filtered. The water layer was extracted with DCM (10 mL Ã 3), and combined organic layers were washed with brine, dried over anhydrous sodium sulfate, filtered, concentrated under reduced pressure, and purified using chromatography on silica gel with DCM/MeOH (20/1â5/1, containing 1% NH3Â·H2O) to give 9 as a yellow solid, with an overall yield of 33% in three steps. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO) Î´ 9.05 (t, J = 5.4 Hz, 1H), 8.87 (d, J = 1.0 Hz, 1H), 8.26 (dd, J = 9.1, 1.7 Hz, 1H), 8.21 (d, J = 9.0 Hz, 1H), 8.14 (s, 1H), 8.11 (d, J = 8.9 Hz, 1H), 7.80 (dd, J = 8.9, 1.4 Hz, 1H), 3.41 (q, J = 12.7, 6.7 Hz, 2H), 2.61 (s, 3H), 2.49 (t, J = 7.3 Hz, 2H), 2.31 (s, 6H), 1.86â1.74 (m, 2H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO) Î´ 165.78, 148.71, 148.62, 140.52, 138.56, 135.00, 133.32, 130.20, 129.84, 129.02, 124.39, 124.18, 123.18, 122.61, 56.99, 45.05, 38.29, 26.92, 22.13. ESI-MS (m/z) 356 [M + H]+.

    Synthesis of Methyl 9-Chloro-7-methylacridine-2-carboxylate (10)

    Based on the procedure for the preparation of 9, with N1,N1-dimethylpropane-1,3-diamine replaced by MeOH, a crude intermediate 10 was obtained and purified using chromatography on silica gel with petroleum ether/ethyl acetate (5/1â1/1) to give 10 as a yellow solid, with an overall yield of 53% in three steps. 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3) Î´ 9.20 (s, 1H), 8.33 (d, J = 9.1 Hz, 1H), 8.23 (d, J = 9.0 Hz, 1H), 8.19 (s, 1H), 8.14 (d, J = 8.8 Hz, 1H), 7.71 (d, J = 8.9 Hz, 1H), 4.04 (s, 3H), 2.64 (s, 3H). ESI-MS (m/z) 286 [M + H]+.

    General Procedure for the Preparation of Acridine Derivatives a5âa28

    To a solution of intermediates 4, 9, or 10 (0.42 mmol, 2.05 equiv) in 2 mL of phenol were added various alkylamines (NH2-L-NH2, 0.2 mmol). The mixture was stirred at 100 Â°C under a nitrogen atmosphere, which was monitored using TCL. After the mixture cooled down, ethyl acetate (20 mL) was added with stirring at 0 Â°C, and then 15 mL of ice-cold 20% NaOH was added dropwise followed by stirring for 20 min. The solution was extracted with ethyl acetate (5 mL Ã 3), and the combined organic phase was washed with 5% NaOH and then brine and dried over anhydrous sodium sulfate.     Biophysical and Biochemical Evaluation Experiments

    Biochemicals and Materials

    All oligomers/primers used in this study were purchased from Invitrogen or Sangon, as shown in Table S1 in the Supporting Information. Stock solutions of all of the derivatives (10 mM) were prepared using DMSO (10%) or double-distilled deionized water. Further dilutions to working concentrations were carried out with double-distilled deionized water or buffer. All other chemicals or solvents were of analytical grade or better.

    Fluorescence Resonance Energy Transfer (FRET) Melting Assay

    The FRET melting assay was carried out on a real-time PCR apparatus following previously published procedures. The following fluorescently dual-labeled oligonucleotides were used as the FRET probes. FPy33T was prepared as a 10 Î¼M solution in 1Ã BPES buffer containing 30 mM (KH2PO4, K2HPO4), 1 mM ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA), and 100 mM KCl (pH 5.5). FPu22T: 5â²-FAM-TGAG GGTGGGTAGGGTGGGTAA-TAMRA-3â² was prepared as a 10 Î¼M solution in Tris-HCl buffer (10 mM, pH 7.4) containing 10 mM KCl. F10T: 5â²-FAM-dTATAGCTATA-HEG-TATAGCTATA-TAMRA-3â², was prepared as 10 Î¼M solution in Tris-HCl buffer (10 mM, pH 7.4) containing 60 mM KCl. The donor fluorophore FAM was 6-carboxyfluorescein, the acceptor fluorophore TAMRA was 6-carboxytetramethylrhodamine, and the HEG linker was [(âCH2âCH2âOâ)6]. These oligonucleotides were thermally annealed. Fluorescence melting curves were determined with a Roche Light Cycler 2 real-time PCR instrument, using a total reaction volume of 20 Î¼L, with 0.2 Î¼M dual-labeled oligonucleotide with or without compounds for FPy33T, FPu22T, and F10T. Fluorescence readings with excitation at 470 nm and detection at 530 nm were taken at intervals of 1 Â°C over the range 37â99 Â°C, with a constant temperature being maintained for 30 s prior to each reading to ensure a stable value. The melting of i-motif, G-quadruplex, and duplex was monitored in the absence or presence of various concentrations of compounds. The final analysis of the data was carried out using Origin8.0 (OriginLab Corp.).

    Surface Plasmon Resonance (SPR) Measurement

    SPR measurements were performed on a ProteOn XPR36 Protein Interaction Array system (Bio-Rad Laboratories, Hercules, CA) using a neutravidin-coated GLH sensor chip. For immobilization, all DNA samples were biotinylated and attached to a reptavidin-coated sensor chip. Oligomer 5â²-biotin Py33 was diluted to 1 Î¼M in running buffer (20 mM 2-(4-morpholino)ethanesulfonic acid, pH 5.8, 100 mM KCl and 0.05% Tween-20), and 5â²-biotin Pu27 and 5â²-biotin duplex DNA were diluted to 1 Î¼M in running buffer (Tris-HCl 50 mM, pH 7.4, 100 mM KCl). The DNA samples were then captured (1000 RU) in flow cells, and a blank cell was set as a control. Different ligand-containing solutions were prepared with the running buffer through serial dilutions from the stock solution (10 mM in DMSO). Six concentrations were injected simultaneously at a flow rate of 25 mL/min for 260 s of the association phase, followed by 300 s of the dissociation phase at 25 Â°C. The GLH sensor chip was regenerated with a short injection of 50 mM NaOH between consecutive measurements. The final graphs were obtained by subtracting blank sensorgrams of i-motif, G-quadruplex or duplex DNA. Data were analyzed with the ProteOn manager software.

    CD Experiments

    CD experiments were performed on a Chirascan circular dichroism spectrophotometer (Applied Photophysics). A quartz cuvette with a 4 mm path length was used for the spectra recorded over a wavelength range of 230â400 nm at 1 nm bandwidth, 1 nm step size, and 0.5 s per point. The oligomer c-myc Py33 or Pu27 was diluted from the stock solution to the required concentration (1 Î¼M) in the absence or presence of compounds (Py33: 1Ã BPES buffer, pH 5.5 or 6.8; Pu27: 10 mM Tris-HCl buffer, pH 7.4) and then annealed by heating at 95 Â°C for 5 min, gradually cooled to room temperature, and stored at 4 Â°C overnight. Spectra were recorded three times over a wavelength range of 230â350 nm, averaged, smoothed, and baseline-corrected to remove the signal contribution from the buffer. The final analysis of the data was carried out using Origin 8.0 (OriginLab Corp.).
    For melting studies, the i-motif or G-quadruplex oligomers of c-myc, bcl-2, c-kit, PDGF, telomere, and VEGF were diluted from the stock solution to 1 Î¼M in the absence or presence of a9, a11, and a26 (i-motif: 1Ã BPES buffer, pH 6.4; G-quadruplex: 10 mM Tris-HCl buffer, 5 mM KCl, pH 7.4) and then annealed by heating at 95 Â°C for 5 min followed by gradual cooling to room temperature. Thermal melting was monitored at 260 nm at the heating rate of 1 Â°C/min. The melting temperatures were determined through curve fitting of melting profiles using the GraphPad Prism software.

    1H NMR

    The NMR samples were prepared in a 10%/90% D2O/H2O solution. The oligomer c-myc Py33 or Pu27 was diluted from the stock solution to 0.5 mM in the absence or presence of a9 (Py33: 1Ã BPES buffer, pH 6.4; Pu27: 10 mM Tris-HCl buffer, pH 7.4), and then annealed by heating at 95 Â°C for 5 min and gradually cooled to room temperature. The stock solution of the compound was dissolved in DMSO-d6. One-dimensional 1H NMR titration experiments were performed on a Bruker DRX-600 MHz spectrometer at temperatures of 25 Â°C, and the water signal was suppressed in the experiment.

    Isothermal Titration Calorimetry (ITC)

    ITC experiments were performed using a VP-ITC titration calorimeter (MicroCal, Northampton, MA). Before being loaded, the solutions were thoroughly degassed. The reference cell was filled with the degassed buffer. The preformed DNA (500 Î¼M Pu27 or 200 Î¼M Py33) was kept in the sample cell, and a syringe with a volume of 300 Î¼L was filled with the ligand a9 (Pu27: 50 Î¼M a9, Py33: 20 Î¼M a9) in the same buffer. The ligand solution was added sequentially in 10 Î¼L aliquots (for a total of 28 injections with a duration of 10 s each) at 4 min intervals at 20 Â°C. In control experiments, the heats of dilution were determined in parallel experiments by injecting a ligand solution of the same concentration in the same buffer. The respective heats of dilution were subtracted from the corresponding binding experiments prior to curve fitting. The thermograms (integrated heat/injection data) obtained in the ITC experiments were fitted with an appropriate model in Origin 7.0.

    Native PAGE Experiments

    Native PAGE experiments were carried out in 1Ã tris-borate-EDTA (TBE) buffer (pH 6.8). The oligomer c-myc Py33 was diluted from the stock solution to the required concentration (3 Î¼M) in 1Ã BPES buffer (pH 6.8) in the absence or presence of different concentrations of compounds a9 and a26 and then annealed by heating at 95 Â°C for 5 min. Then these oligomers were gradually cooled to room temperature and incubated at 4 Â°C overnight. Electrophoresis was carried out using 20% acrylamide (pH 6.6) at 140 V for 5 h at 5 Â°C. The gels were then silver-stained.

    Cell Culture

    The human melanoma cell line A375, the human squamous cervical cancer cell line Hela, the human lung adenocarcinoma cell line A549, the human bone osteosarcoma epithelial cell line U2OS, the human colon cancer cell line HCT116, the human cervical cancer cell line SiHa, and the human hepatocellular carcinoma cell line HuH7 were purchased from the China Center for Type Culture Collection in Wuhan. The cell lines were maintained in the RPMI-1640 or DMEM medium supplemented with 10% fetal calf serum at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2.

    MTT Cytotoxicity Assay

    A375, Hela, A549, U2OS, HCT-116, SiHa, and HuH7 cells were seeded on 96-well plates (5.0 Ã 103 per well) with 100 Î¼L of culture medium and incubated for 12 h at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2. The cells were incubated in the presence or absence of the indicated concentrations of compounds, and the control group was administered the same volume of dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO). After incubation for 48 h, 20 Î¼L of 2.5 mg/mL a methyl thiazolyl tetrazolium (MTT) solution was added to each well and further incubated for 4 h. The cells in each well were then treated with dimethyl sulfoxide (200 Î¼L) after the culture medium was siphoned off, and the absorbance was recorded at 570 nm. All drug doses were parallel tested in triplicate, and the cytotoxicity was evaluated based on the percentage of cell survival in a dose-dependent manner, deducting the negative control. The final IC50 values were calculated using the Graph Pad Prism 6.

    Dual-Luciferase Reporter Assay

    In this assay, 200 ng of the constructed psiCHECK2 luciferase plasmid (Promega) containing c-myc wild-type, mutant, or deleted promoters was transfected into SiHa cells using Lipofectamine 3000 (Invitrogen). After 8 h, compounds were added to the cells. The cells were incubated at 37 Â°C with CO2 for 48 h, and the transfected cells were first washed with ice-cold 1Ã phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) buffer to reduce the background signals from the medium. Luciferase assays were subsequently performed according to the manufacturerâs instructions using the dual-luciferase assay system (Promega). After a 3 s delay, secreted luciferase signals were collected for 10 s using a microplate reader (Molecular Devices, Flex Station 3). The quantification was performed using a multimode reader (Molecular Devices). The secreted Renilla luciferase activity was normalized to the firefly luciferase activity.

    RNA Extraction and Reverse Transcription Polymerase Chain Reaction (RT-PCR)

    SiHa cells were seeded in a 6-well plate (2 Ã 105 cells/well) and incubated for 12 h at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2. The cells were incubated in the presence or absence of different concentrations of a9 and a26, and the control group was administered the same volume of DMSO. After incubation for 24 h, the harvested cells were washed with 1Ã PBS (pH 7.4) and lysed in a TRIzol solution. The total RNA was extracted according to the protocol supplied by the Takara Company and eluted with distilled, deionized water containing 0.1% diethyl pyrocarbonate (DEPC) to give a final volume of 30 Î¼L. RNA was quantified spectrophotometrically. The total RNA was used as a template for reverse transcription according to the following protocol: each 20 Î¼L reaction mixture contained 1Ã M-MLV buffer, 500 Î¼M dNTP, 100 pmol oligo dT18 primer, 100 units of M-MLV reverse transcriptase, DEPC-H2O, and 1.5 Î¼g of total RNA. The mixtures were incubated at 42 Â°C for 60 min for reverse transcription and then at 70 Â°C for 15 min. PCR was performed according to the following protocol: each 20 Î¼L reaction mixture contained 1Ã PCR buffer, 500 Î¼M dNTPs, 0.2 Î¼M primer pairs, 1 unit Taq polymerase, 0.1% DEPC-H2O, and 2 Î¼L of the cDNA template. The reactions were performed in a Master-cycler Personal (Eppendorf) according to the following protocol: 95 Â°C for 5 min, 30 cycles of 95 Â°C for 1 min, 58 Â°C for 30 s, and 72 Â°C for 1 min. The amplified products were separated on 1.2% agarose gel and photographed using a Gel Doc 2000 Imager System.

    RNA Stability Assay

    SiHa cells were seeded in a 6-well plate (2 Ã 105/well) and incubated for 24 h at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2. The cells were incubated in the presence of 5 Î¼g/mL actinomycin D or 5 Î¼g/mL actinomycin D plus 2.5 Î¼M a9. Total RNA was extracted according to the protocol mentioned by the Takara Company at the indicated time points following the addition of actinomycin D. The reverse transcription and PCR processes were performed using normal RT-PCR methods. The transcript turnover rates were then calculated based on the nonlinear fitting one-phase exponential decay curves using the GraphPad Prism software, and were generally expressed as times to 50% mRNA decay for each experiment.

    Western Blotting

    After SiHa cells were taken board for each well of 2 Ã 105 and then treated with different concentrations of a9 and a26 for 24 h, the cells harvested from each well of culture plates were lysed in 200 Î¼L of protein extraction buffer consisting of 1 mM PMSF for 30 min. The suspension was centrifuged at 10â¯000 rpm at 4 Â°C for 15 min, and the protein content of the supernatant was measured using the bicinchoninic acid (BCA) assay. The same amount of protein for each sample was loaded onto 8% polyacrylamide gel and then transferred to a microporous poly(vinylidene difluoride) (PVDF) membrane. Western blotting was performed using anti-c-MYC and anti-Î²-actin (cell signaling technology) antibodies, as well as the horseradish peroxidase-conjugated anti-rabbit secondary antibody. Protein bands were visualized using a chemiluminescence substrate.

    Colony Formation Assay

    SiHa cells were subsequently seeded in 6-well culture plates (500/well) for a 24 h pre-culture at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2 and then treated with a9 at different concentrations for 9 days. The cells were washed with 1Ã PBS and fixed with ice-cold methanol for 10 min, followed by the addition of a 0.5% crystal violet solution for 30 min to observe the colony formation. Finally, the plates were washed with water, dried, and photographed.

    Annexin V-FITC Apoptosis Detection

    SiHa cells were seeded in a 6-well plate (2 Ã 105/well) and incubated for 24 h at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2. After being incubated in the presence of different concentrations of a9 and the control group was administered the same volume of DMSO for 24 h, the SiHa cells were washed with PBS, centrifuged, and resuspended in an Annexin V-FITC solution for 15 min at room temperature in dark. After being centrifuged for 5 min, the cells were then resuspended in an Annexin V-FITC solution and mixed with a PI staining solution for 10 min at 2â5 Â°C in dark. Then, the cells were analyzed using flow cytometry with an Epics Elite flow cytometer (Beckman Coulter).

    Cell Cycle Analysis

    SiHa cells were seeded in a 6-well plate (2 Ã 105/well) and incubated for 24 h at 37 Â°C in a humidified atmosphere with 5% CO2. After being incubated in the presence of different concentrations of a9 and the control group was administered the same volume of DMSO for 24 h, the SiHa cells were then washed in PBS. The cells were harvested and fixed in ice-cold 70% ethanol overnight. The cells were centrifuged and resuspended in 1 mL of PBS containing 20 Î¼g/mL RNase A at 37 Â°C for 30 min. The cells were then incubated with the DNA staining PI solution, and flow cytometry analysis was carried out using Epics Elite flow cytometry (Beckman Coulter, Epics XL) carried out at 488 nm. The data were analyzed using the EXPO32 ADC analysis software.

    Evaluation of In Vivo Antitumor Activity

    BALB/c female nude mice were obtained from the Laboratory Animal Center of Sun Yat-sen University. All procedures were approved by the Animal Care and Use Committee of Sun Yat-sen University and conformed to the legal mandates and national guidelines for the care and maintenance of laboratory animals. SiHa cells were harvested, pelleted through centrifugation at 1000 rpm for 5 min, and resuspended in sterile serum-free medium without EDTA. The cells (1 Ã 107 in 100 Î¼L) were then subcutaneously implanted into the underarm regions of five mice. After the tumors grew to almost 1000 mm3, the tumor tissues were removed and divided. Then, the divided tissues were implanted into the underarm of 18 mice. These mice were separated into three groups: negative control, a9-treated, and positive control (cisplatin-treated). a9, cisplatin, and saline were administered by ip injection to athymic nude mice with human tumor xenografts established using SiHa cervical cancer cells. Mice were injected ip once 2 days for 18 days. The positive control group received cisplatin at a dose of 0.2 mg/kg, and a9 was similarly administered to mice at a dose of 15 mg/kg. After treatment for 18 days, the animals were sacrificed and the organs of the mice were collected and weighed.

      The spliceosome is a therapeutic vulnerability in MYC-driven cancer
      Hsu, Tiffany Y.-T.; Simon, Lukas M.; Neill, Nicholas J.; Marcotte, Richard; Sayad, Azin; Bland, Christopher S.; Echeverria, Gloria V.; Sun, Tingting; Kurley, Sarah J.; Tyagi, Siddhartha; Karlin, Kristen L.; Dominguez-Vidana, Rocio; Hartman, Jessica D.; Renwick, Alexander; Scorsone, Kathleen; Bernardi, Ronald J.; Skinner, Samuel O.; Jain, Antrix; Orellana, Mayra; Lagisetti, Chandraiah; Golding, Ido; Jung, Sung Y.; Neilson, Joel R.; Zhang, Xiang H.-F.; Cooper, Thomas A.; Webb, Thomas R.; Neel, Benjamin G.; Shaw, Chad A.; Westbrook, Thomas F.
      Nature (London, United Kingdom) (2015), 525 (7569), 384-388CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836. (Nature Publishing Group)
      MYC (also known as c-MYC) overexpression or hyperactivation is one of the most common drivers of human cancer. Despite intensive study, the MYC oncogene remains recalcitrant to therapeutic inhibition. MYC is a transcription factor, and many of its pro-tumorigenic functions have been attributed to its ability to regulate gene expression programs. Notably, oncogenic MYC activation has also been shown to increase total RNA and protein prodn. in many tissue and disease contexts. While such increases in RNA and protein prodn. may endow cancer cells with pro-tumor hallmarks, this increase in synthesis may also generate new or heightened burden on MYC-driven cancer cells to process these macromols. properly. Here we discover that the spliceosome is a new target of oncogenic stress in MYC-driven cancers. We identify BUD31 as a MYC-synthetic lethal gene in human mammary epithelial cells, and demonstrate that BUD31 is a component of the core spliceosome required for its assembly and catalytic activity. Core spliceosomal factors (such as SF3B1 and U2AF1) assocd. with BUD31 are also required to tolerate oncogenic MYC. Notably, MYC hyperactivation induces an increase in total precursor mRNA synthesis, suggesting an increased burden on the core spliceosome to process pre-mRNA. In contrast to normal cells, partial inhibition of the spliceosome in MYC-hyperactivated cells leads to global intron retention, widespread defects in pre-mRNA maturation, and deregulation of many essential cell processes. Notably, genetic or pharmacol. inhibition of the spliceosome in vivo impairs survival, tumorigenicity and metastatic proclivity of MYC-dependent breast cancers. Collectively, these data suggest that oncogenic MYC confers a collateral stress on splicing, and that components of the spliceosome may be therapeutic entry points for aggressive MYC-driven cancers.

      Small molecules targeting c-Myc oncogene: promising anti-cancer therapeutics
      Chen, Bing-Jia; Wu, Yan-Ling; Tanaka, Yoshimasa; Zhang, Wen
      International Journal of Biological Sciences (2014), 10 (10), 1084-1096, 13 pp.CODEN: IJBSB9; ISSN:1449-2288. (Ivyspring International Publisher)
      A review. The nuclear transcription factor c-Myc is a member of the Myc gene family with multiple functions and located on band q24.1 of chromosome 8. The c-Myc gene is activated by chromosomal translocation, rearrangement, and amplification. Its encoded protein transduces intracellular signals to the nucleus, resulting in the regulation of cell proliferation, differentiation, and apoptosis, and has the ability to transform cells and bind chromosomal DNA. c-Myc also plays a crit. role in malignant transformation. The abnormal over-expression of c-Myc is frequently obsd. in some tumors, including carcinomas of the breast, colon, and cervix, as well as small-cell lung cancer, osteosarcomas, glioblastomas, and myeloid leukemias, therefore making it a possible target for anticancer therapy. In this minireview, we summarize unique characteristics of c-Myc and therapeutic strategies against cancer using small mols. targeting the oncogene, and discuss the prospects in the development of agents targeting c-Myc, in particular G-quadruplexes formed in c-Myc promoter and c-Myc/Max dimerization. Such information will be of importance for the research and development of c-Myc-targeted drugs.

      Inhibition of c-Myc expression accounts for an increase in the number of multinucleated cells in human cervical epithelial cells
      Cui Feng Mei; Huang Cheng Cheng; Chen Qiu; Song Man; Zhou Ping Kun; Cui Feng Mei; Chen Qiu; Sun Xiu Jin; He Yong Ming; Zhang Shi Meng; Guan Hua; Zhou Ping Kun; Hou Jun
      Oncology letters (2017), 14 (3), 2878-2886 ISSN:1792-1074.
      The present study aimed to explore the mechanisms by which c-Myc is involved in mitotic catastrophe. HeLa-630 is a cell line stably silenced for c-Myc expression that was established in the laboratory of the School of Radiation Medicine and Protection. Multinucleated cells were observed in this line, and gene expression analysis was utilized to examine differences in gene expression in these cells compared with in the control cells transfected with the control plasmid. Gene ontology analysis was performed for differentially expressed genes. Expression profile analyses revealed that cells with silenced c-Myc exhibited abnormal expression patterns of genes involved in various functions, including the regulation of microtubule nucleation, centrosome duplication, the formation of pericentriolar material, DNA synthesis and metabolism, protein metabolism and the regulation of ion concentrations. Pathway analyses of differentially expressed genes demonstrated that these genes were primarily involved in diverse signal transduction pathways, including not only the adherens junction pathway, the transforming growth factor-Î² signaling pathway and the Wnt signaling pathway, among others, but also signaling pathways with roles in cytokine and immune regulation. The proportion of multinucleated cells with multipolar spindles was significantly higher in silenced c-Myc cells as compared with the control cells, and this discrepancy became more pronounced following cell irradiation. The inhibition of c-Myc in tumors may account for the radiosensitization of certain tumor cell types.

      Function of the c-Myc Oncogenic Transcription Factor
      Dang, Chi V.; Resar, Linda M. S.; Emison, Eileen; Kim, Sunkyu; Li, Qing; Prescott, Julia E.; Wonsey, Diane; Zeller, Karen
      Experimental Cell Research (1999), 253 (1), 63-77CODEN: ECREAL; ISSN:0014-4827. (Academic Press)
      A review with 238 refs. The c-myc gene and the expression of the c-Myc protein are frequently altered in human cancers. The c-myc gene encodes the transcription factor c-Myc, which heterodimerizes with a partner protein, termed Max, to regulate gene expression. Max also heterodimerizes with the Mad family of proteins to repress transcription, antagonize c-Myc, and promote cellular differentiation. The constitutive activation of c-myc expression is key to the genesis of many cancers, and hence the understanding of c-Myc function depends on our understanding of its target genes. In this review, the authors attempt to place the putative target genes of c-Myc in the context of c-Myc-mediated phenotypes. From this perspective, c-Myc emerges as an oncogenic transcription factor that integrates the cell cycle machinery with cell adhesion, cellular metab., and the apoptotic pathways. (c) 1999 Academic Press.

      Transcriptional amplification in tumor cells with elevated c-Myc
      Lin, Charles Y.; Loven, Jakob; Rahl, Peter B.; Paranal, Ronald M.; Burge, Christopher B.; Bradner, James E.; Lee, Tong Ihn; Young, Richard A.
      Cell (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2012), 151 (1), 56-67CODEN: CELLB5; ISSN:0092-8674. (Cell Press)
      Elevated expression of the c-Myc transcription factor occurs frequently in human cancers and is assocd. with tumor aggression and poor clin. outcome. The effect of high levels of c-Myc on global gene regulation is poorly understood but is widely thought to involve newly activated or repressed "Myc target genes." We report here that in tumor cells expressing high levels of c-Myc the transcription factor accumulates in the promoter regions of active genes and causes transcriptional amplification, producing increased levels of transcripts within the cell's gene expression program. Thus, rather than binding and regulating a new set of genes, c-Myc amplifies the output of the existing gene expression program. These results provide an explanation for the diverse effects of oncogenic c-Myc on gene expression in different tumor cells and suggest that transcriptional amplification reduces rate-limiting constraints for tumor cell growth and proliferation.

      Dihydroorotate dehydrogenase Inhibitors Target c-Myc and Arrest Melanoma, Myeloma and Lymphoma cells at S-phase
      Dorasamy Mathura Subangari; Wong Pooi-Fong; Dorasamy Mathura Subangari; Choudhary Bhavesh; Nellore Kavitha; Subramanya Hosahalli
      Journal of Cancer (2017), 8 (15), 3086-3098 ISSN:1837-9664.
      Dihydroorotate dehydrogenase (DHODH) is a rate-limiting enzyme in the de novo biosynthesis pathway of pyrimidines. Inhibition of this enzyme impedes cancer cell proliferation but the exact mechanisms of action of these inhibitors in cancer cells are poorly understood. In this study, we showed that cancer cells, namely melanoma, myeloma and lymphoma overexpressed DHODH protein and treatment with A771726 and Brequinar sodium resulted in cell cycle arrest at S-phase. Transfection with DHODH shRNA depleted DHODH protein expression and impeded the proliferation of melanoma cells. shRNA knockdown of DHODH in combination with DHODH inhibitors further reduced the cancer cell proliferation, suggesting that knockdown of DHODH had sensitized the cells to DHODH inhibitors. Cell cycle regulatory proteins, c-Myc and its transcriptional target, p21 were found down- and up-regulated, respectively, following treatment with DHODH inhibitors in melanoma, myeloma and lymphoma cells. Interestingly, knockdown of DHODH by shRNA had also similarly affected the expression of c-Myc and p21 proteins. Our findings suggest that DHODH inhibitors induce cell cycle arrest in cancer cells via additional DHODH-independent pathway that is associated with p21 up-regulation and c-Myc down-regulation. Hence, DHODH inhibitors can be explored as potential therapeutic agents in cancer therapy.

      X-ray crystallographic validation of structure predictions used in computational design for protein stabilization
      Floor, Robert J.; Wijma, Hein J.; Jekel, Peter A.; Terwisscha van Scheltinga, Anke C.; Dijkstra, Bauke W.; Janssen, Dick B.
      Proteins: Structure, Function, and Bioinformatics (2015), 83 (5), 940-951CODEN: PSFBAF ISSN:. (Wiley-Blackwell)

The structures also revealed how a c-Myc tag, which was introduced for facile detection and purifn., could reduce access to the active site and thereby lower the catalytic activity.       I-motif structures formed in the human c-MYC promoter are highly dynamic--insights into sequence redundancy and I-motif stability
      Dai Jixun; Hatzakis Emmanuel; Hurley Laurence H; Yang Danzhou
      PloS one (2010), 5 (7), e11647 ISSN:.
      The GC-rich nuclease hypersensitivity element III1 (NHE III1) of the c-MYC promoter largely controls the transcriptional activity of the c-MYC oncogene. The C-rich strand in this region can form I-motif DNA secondary structures. We determined the folding pattern of the major I-motif formed in the NHE III1, which can be formed at near-neutral pH. While we find that the I-motif formed in the four 3' consecutive runs of cytosines appears to be the most favored, our results demonstrate that the C-rich strand of the c-MYC NHE III1 exhibits a high degree of dynamic equilibration. Using a trisubstituted oligomer of this region, we determined the formation of two equilibrating loop isomers, one of which contains a flipped-out cytosine. Our results indicate that the intercalative cytosine+-cytosine base pairs are not always necessary for an intramolecular I-motif. The dynamic character of the c-MYC I-motif is intrinsic to the NHE III1 sequence and appears to provide stability to the c-MYC I-motif.
